SUMMARY OF FINDINGS AND RECOMMENDATIONS 
Our borings generally encountered a surface fill layer about 0.5 to 8 feet thick 
underlain by residual soil, saprolite and basalt rock formation extending to the maximum 
depth explored of about 11.5 feet below the existing pavement surface. In one of the borings, 
alluvium was encountered from about 0.5 to 9 feet depths. We did not encounter 
groundwater in the drilled borings at the time of our field exploration.  It should be noted that 
groundwater levels are subject to change due to rainfall, time of year, seasonal precipitation, 
surface water runoff, and other factors. 
We anticipate that the new water main inverts will be about 4 to 5 feet below the 
existing pavement surface. However, deeper excavations may be required where the new 
water main traverses under existing utilities. Installation of the proposed water mains will 
involve excavation into the underlying fills, residual and saprolitic soils, cobbles, boulders, 
and basalt formation. In general, we anticipate that the in-situ soils along the proposed 
water main alignments may be excavated with normal heavy excavation equipment. 
However, trench excavations into the underlying basalt rock formation and/or cobbles and 
boulders may require chipping and/or the use of hoerams to remove the materials and to 
achieve the design invert elevations. 
Where deep excavations (greater than 5 feet in depth) are planned, temporary 
shoring or sloping and benching should be implemented for the trench excavations. 
Based on our site observations, we believe that temporary cut slopes for open cut 
excavations are not feasible. Therefore, trench walls would have to be cut near vertical, 
necessitating the use of temporary shoring during construction.  
In general, the excavated on-site soils encountered during our field exploration may 
be used in the upper portion of the trench backfill from a level 12 inches above the pipes to 
the pavement subgrade, provided that the materials are free of vegetation, deleterious 
materials, organic material, and clay lumps and rock fragments greater than 3 inches in 
maximum dimension. 
Design of metallic substructures, such as waterline pipes or other metallic piping, 
should consider the effects of the corrosive environment on the substructure. Based on 
the laboratory resistivity results, the subsurface soils at the project site have resistivity 
values ranging from about 550 to 4,800 ohm-cm and are considered Category A 
(Moderately to Severely Corrosive) based on the updated Board of Water Supply External 
Corrosion Control Standards dated July 28, 2021. It should be noted that the resistivity 
values of the subsurface soils varied substantially with depth and location across the 
SUMMARY OF FINDINGS AND RECOMMENDATIONS 
W.O. 8155-00 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page iv 
Hawaii • California 
project area; therefore, special attention should be given by the corrosion engineer when 
using the resistivity results provided in this report. 
The text of this report should be referred to for detailed discussion and specific 
recommendations for the installation of the new waterlines. 
END OF SUMMARY OF FINDINGS AND RECOMMENDATIONS

SECTION 1.  GENERAL 
1.1 
Introduction 
This report presents the results of our geotechnical engineering exploration 
performed for the Leolua Street and Kipou Street Area Water System Improvements in 
the Waipahu area on the Island of Oahu, Hawaii. The project location and general vicinity 
are shown on the Project Location Map, Plate 1. 
This report summarizes the findings and presents our geotechnical engineering 
recommendations derived from our field exploration, laboratory testing, and engineering 
analyses for the water system improvements project. These recommendations are 
intended for the design of trench excavation, backfilling, pavement restoration, thrust 
block/vault design, and corrosion potential only. The findings and recommendations 
presented herein are subject to the limitations noted at the end of this report. 
1.2 
Project Considerations 
The Leolua Street and Kipou Street Area Water System Improvements project is 
located north of Farrington Highway and east of Fort Weaver Road in the Waipahu area. 
The project is part of the Pearl Harbor 228 and Kunia 440 systems. The project consists 
of the installation of approximately 12,450 linear feet of waterline replacement with pipe 
diameters of 4, 8, and 12 inches along the following roadways: Leolua Street, Leoku 
Street, Hoaeae Street, Loaa Street, Haaa Street, Kipou Street, Kipou Place, Kiokio Place, 
Kiwini Place, Hene Street, Kenola Place, and Hulahe Street.  
The project includes rehabilitation and improvements to existing waterline systems 
for a total installation of about 12,450 linear feet of 4, 8, and 12-inch diameter waterlines 
planned for the source water system, designated as Pearl Harbor 228 and Kunia 440, as 
summarized below:

The scope of work requires that the system be designed in accordance with the 
Honolulu Board of Water Supply (BWS) standards, including corrosion control. 
Based on the Board of Water Supply standards, corrosion testing will be performed 
at every 300-foot interval. For this project, a minimum of about 47 tests would be required. 
We anticipate that the new waterline pipe invert will be about 4 to 5 feet from the existing 
pavement surface.

subsurface soil conditions along the new waterline alignments to develop an idealized 
soil/rock data set to formulate geotechnical engineering recommendations for the

SECTION 2.  SITE CHARACTERIZATION 
2.1 
Regional Geology 
The Island of Oahu was built by the extrusion of basaltic lava from the Waianae 
and Koolau Shield Volcanoes. The older Waianae Volcano is estimated to be middle to 
late Pliocene in age, and Koolau Volcano is estimated to be late Pliocene to early 
Pleistocene in age. As volcanic activity at Waianae Volcano ceased, lava flows from 
Koolau Volcano banked against its eroded eastern slope forming the Schofield Plateau. 
The project site is located at the southern lower flank of the Koolau Volcano. 
The Koolau Volcanic Shield was built during the late Pliocene Epoch and early 
Pleistocene Epoch by the extrusion of successive thinly bedded lava flows. The main 
shield-building stage ceased approximately 2.5 million years ago. Evidence from historic 
drilled wells indicates that the Island of Oahu has subsided by as much as 1,200 feet 
since the cessation of the early volcanic activity (Macdonald and Abbott, 1970). During 
the period of island subsidence, coral-algal reefs began to grow along the southern coast 
of Oahu forming embayments protected by barrier reefs. A series of lagoons formed 
behind the barrier reefs and both terrigenous and marine sediments accumulated in the 
lagoons (Macdonald and Abbott, 1970). 
During the Pleistocene Epoch (Ice Age), many sea level changes occurred as a 
result of widespread glaciation in the continental areas of the world. As the great 
continental glaciers accumulated, the level of the oceans fell because there was less 
water available to fill the oceanic basins. Conversely, as the glaciers receded (melted), 
global sea levels rose because the volume of water increased. The land mass comprising 
the Island of Oahu remained essentially stable during these water level changes, and the 
fluctuations were eustatic in nature. These glacio-eustatic fluctuations resulted in stands 
of the sea that were both higher and lower relative to present sea level on the Island of 
Oahu.  
The higher sea level stands caused landform changes, including the accumulation 
of deltas and alluvial fans composed of terrigenous sediments in the heads of the old 
bays, the accumulation of reef deposits at correspondingly higher elevations, and the 
deposition of lagoonal and/or marine sediments in the quiet lagoonal waters protected by 
SECTION 2. SITE CHARACTERIZATION  
W.O. 8155-00 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 6 
Hawaii • California 
barrier reefs. The concurrent growth of reefs and the accumulation of lagoonal sediments 
also resulted in the deposition of coral algal limestone and marl materials within the 
predominantly lagoonal sedimentary unit. 
The lower sea level stands caused streams to carve drainages into the coastal 
plain platforms composed of sedimentary and coral reef deposits. In addition, subaerial 
exposure of the calcareous sediments caused consolidation of the soft deltaic materials 
and lagoonal deposits and the induration of calcareous reef materials. Furthermore, 
renewed subaerial erosion acting at the upper elevations of the volcanic shield caused 
the downstream deposition of terrigenous alluvial sediments under relatively higher 
energy conditions.  
During periods of no significant sea level changes, continued meandering stream 
action extended the alluvial deltas and fans seaward and deposited alluvial materials 
overlying the marine-lagoonal sediments.  
The higher sea level stands caused the formation of deltas and fans of 
accumulated terrigenous sediments in the heads of old bays, accumulated reef deposits 
at correspondingly higher elevations, and deposition of lagoonal/marine sediments in 
quiet waters protected by fringing reefs. The lower sea stands caused streams to carve 
valleys in the sediments and reef deposits. Subaerial exposure of the sediments and 
calcareous materials caused consolidation of the soft deltaic materials and lagoonal 
deposits and induration of the calcareous reef materials, or coral detritus.  
2.2 
Existing Site Conditions 
The project site is located in the lower Waipahu area on the Island of Oahu, Hawaii. 
It is planned to install waterline mains, fire hydrants, service laterals, thrust blocks, 
concrete valve vaults and other appurtenances along several streets within residential 
subdivisions. The existing roadways within the project are two lanes residential streets 
and are primarily paved with asphaltic concrete. The existing asphaltic concrete was 
observed to be in fair condition. Overhead utility lines were observed on Hoaeae Street, 
Loaa Street, Haaa Street, Leoku Street, and Leolua Street. In general, the topography of 
the project site slopes toward the ocean (northwest to southeast) with the roadways being 
fairly level. Based on the topographic survey map provided, the existing top-of-pavement 
SECTION 2. SITE CHARACTERIZATION  
W.O. 8155-00 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 7 
Hawaii • California 
elevations range from approximately +157 feet Mean Sea Level (MSL) along Kipou Street 
to about +34 feet MSL along Leoku Street. 
2.3 
Subsurface Conditions 
Our field exploration consisted of drilling and sampling 47 borings, designated as 
Boring Nos. 1 through 47, extending to depths of about 10 to 11.5 feet below the existing 
ground surface. In addition, five bulk samples of the near-surface soils, designated as 
Bulk-1 through Bulk-5, were obtained to evaluate the pavement support characteristics of 
the near-surface soils. The approximate boring and bulk sample locations are shown on 
the Overall Site Plan, Plate 2, and Site Plans, Plates 3.1 through 3.4.  
Our field exploration generally encountered a surface fill layer about 0.5 to 8 feet 
thick underlain by residual soil, saprolite and basalt rock formation extending to the 
maximum depth explored of about 11.5 feet below the existing pavement surface. In one 
of the borings, alluvium was encountered from about 0.5 to 9 feet depths. 
The surface fill layer consisted of 3 to 12 inches of asphaltic concrete; medium 
dense to dense sandy gravel, gravelly sand/clay, and silty/clayey sand; stiff sandy silt; 
and cobbles and boulders. 
The residual soil consisted of medium stiff to hard silty/sandy clay and 
clayey/sandy silt. The saprolite was composed of dense to very dense silty/gravelly sand 
and sandy gravel. The basalt rock formation was hard. The alluvium consisted of soft to 
stiff silty clay. 
We did not encounter groundwater in the drilled borings at the time of our field 
exploration. It should be noted that groundwater levels are subject to change due to 
rainfall, time of year, seasonal precipitation, surface water runoff, perched groundwater, 
and other factors.

SECTION 3.  DISCUSSION AND RECOMMENDATIONS 
Our field exploration generally encountered a surface fill layer about 0.5 to 8 feet 
thick underlain by residual and saprolitic soils, alluvium, and basalt formation extending 
to the maximum depth explored of about 11.5 feet below the existing pavement surface. 
Basalt formation was encountered in three of our borings. We did not encounter 
groundwater in the drilled borings at the time of our field exploration. 
We understand that the construction of the water mains will generally consist of 
trench excavation, pipe placement, trench backfill, and pavement restoration. Detailed 
discussions for the design of the water system improvements project, including trench 
excavation, trench excavation support, backfilling, pavement restoration, thrust 
block/vault design, corrosion protection, and other geotechnical aspects of the project are 
presented in the following sections. 
3.1 
Trench Excavation 
We anticipate that the invert elevations of 8 and 12-inch diameter water main pipes 
will be about 4 to 5 feet below the existing pavement surface. Installation of the proposed 
water mains will involve excavation into the underlying fills, residual and saprolitic soils, 
alluvium, and hard basalt rock formation. In general, we anticipate that the in-situ soils 
along the proposed water main alignments may be excavated with normal heavy 
excavation equipment. 
As mentioned above, our field exploration indicated that hard basalt was 
encountered at relatively shallow depths in two of our borings. In addition, cobbles and 
boulders were encountered in one of our borings at about 6 feet below the existing 
pavement surface. Therefore, excavations extending into basalt rock formation and 
cobbles and boulders within the trench depths should be anticipated. Trench excavations 
into the basalt rock formation and cobbles and boulders may require chipping and/or the 
use of hoerams to remove the materials and achieve the design invert elevations. The 
contractor must exercise care to avoid over-ripping, which would disrupt the structure of 
the rock formation, which may result in a potential loss of bearing strength for foundations 
in its vicinity. 
SECTION 3. DISCUSSION AND RECOMMENDATIONS  
W.O. 8155-00 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 10 
Hawaii • California 
The above discussion regarding the excavation and rippability of the subsurface 
materials is based on field data obtained from the borings drilled at the project site. We 
recommend encouraging contractors bidding on this project to examine the site conditions 
and soil/rock data to make their own interpretation. 
3.2 
Trench Excavation Support 
We anticipate that excavations up to about 5 to 6 feet below the pavement surface 
will be required for the installation of the new water mains. Deeper excavations may be 
required where the water mains traverse existing underground utilities. Where deeper 
excavations (greater than 5 feet in depth) are planned, temporary shoring or sloping and 
benching should be implemented for the trench excavations. Based on our site 
observations, we believe that temporary cut slopes for open cut excavations are not 
feasible. Therefore, trench walls would have to be cut near vertical, necessitating the use 
of temporary shoring during construction. 
The excavation support and shoring system used must comply with applicable 
safety requirements. The contractor should be solely responsible for the adequacy and 
safety of the shoring installation. The contractor’s representative should be on-site at all 
times during excavation and construction work for the opportunity to promptly observe 
changing or unforeseen conditions, such as high groundwater, inappropriate construction 
sequence or techniques, etc., which may affect the shoring stability. 
Excavated soils should not be stockpiled closer than a horizontal distance equal 
to the depth of the excavation from the edge of the excavation to reduce the potential for 
excessive ground movement. 
It is important to install adequate shoring and to maintain it tight against the 
excavation walls with proper bracing during construction. The properly braced shoring is 
essential to reduce the potential for appreciable lateral movements of the adjacent ground 
into the excavation, which may result in potential settlement or distress to adjacent 
structures or other improvements.  
It must be noted that some minor movements of the shoring system and the 
adjacent ground may still occur due to changes in earth stresses during excavation. Due 
SECTION 3. DISCUSSION AND RECOMMENDATIONS  
W.O. 8155-00 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 11 
Hawaii • California 
to the complexity of the stress changes, it is difficult to accurately estimate the magnitude 
of movement. The magnitude also depends greatly upon workmanship, such as how 
quickly and tightly the shoring and bracing supports are installed, the subsurface 
conditions, the size of the excavation, and the rate of excavation. 
Therefore, it is important to realize that the excavation shoring should be installed 
properly and as early as practical. The adjacent ground should be continuously monitored 
for cracks, dips, and/or other indications of movements with instruments until the 
subsurface water system pipes are finally backfilled. 
3.3 
Pipe Cushion 
The stress distribution against the bottom of a pipe has a significant effect on the 
load supporting capacity of the pipe. Therefore, the pipe cushion is an important design 
consideration. For pipes bearing on the underlying fills, residual and saprolitic soils, 
alluvium, and hard basalt rock formation, a minimum of 6 inches of granular cushion 
material, such as No. 3B Fine gravel (ASTM C33, No. 67 Gradation) should be provided 
below the pipe. Where soft and/or loose soils are encountered at or near the invert of the 
pipes, a stabilization layer consisting of an additional 18 inches of open-graded gravel, 
such as No. 3B Fine gravel, wrapped in a non-woven filter fabric (Mirafi 180N or 
equivalent) should be provided below the cushion layer for uniform support. 
The granular cushion layer should extend beyond the pipe sides a minimum width 
of one-fourth (¼) the outside pipe diameter or trench width, whichever is greater, and 
should extend 12 inches above the pipe. Before the placement of cushion material, a 
Geolabs representative should observe the excavated trench bottom to confirm that firm 
materials are exposed at the bottom of the trench or whether a stabilization layer should 
be provided below the cushion layer. 
3.4 
Trench Backfill 
In general, the trench backfill should be placed in accordance with the Water 
System Standards, State of Hawaii 2002 revision. The first zone of backfill extending from 
the cushion material to at least 12 inches above the top of the pipes should consist of 
select free-draining granular material (ASTM C33, No. 67 Gradation) to reduce the 
compaction effort required and resulting stresses on the pipe. In addition, it is critical to 
SECTION 3. DISCUSSION AND RECOMMENDATIONS  
W.O. 8155-00 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 12 
Hawaii • California 
use this free-draining material to reduce the potential for the formation of voids below the 
haunches of pipes and to provide adequate support for the sides of the pipes. Improper 
trench backfill could result in backfill settlement and pipe damage. 
The upper portion of the trench backfill from a level 12 inches above the pipes to 
the pavement subgrade should consist of the excavated on-site soils or imported fill 
materials that are free of vegetation, deleterious materials, organic material, and clay 
lumps and rock fragments greater than 3 inches in maximum dimension. It should be 
noted that the fill requirements presented herein are intended as guidelines only and may 
be modified based on Geolabs additional laboratory testing and field observations on the 
available fill materials. 
Imported fill materials used for trench backfill should be well-graded from coarse 
to fine with particles no larger than 3 inches in largest dimension. The material should 
contain between 10 and 30 percent particles passing the No. 200 sieve. The material 
should have a laboratory California Bearing Ratio (CBR) value of 20 or higher and a swell 
potential of 1 percent or less when tested in accordance with ASTM D1883. Geolabs 
should test the imported fill materials for conformance with these recommendations. 
As an alternative, to facilitate the backfilling and reduce the time required for 
applying compactive effort, Controlled Low Strength Material (CLSM) may be used to 
backfill the trenches. The use of CLSM would also reduce the potential for future 
settlement of the trench backfill under the repeated wheel loading from traffic on the 
roadway. 
Aggregate base course materials required for the flexible pavement restoration 
section should consist of crushed basaltic aggregates and should conform to Section 31 
of the City and County of Honolulu Standard Specifications (September 1986). Geolabs 
should test the imported fill materials for conformance with these recommendations. 
3.5 
Trench Backfill Compaction and Placement Requirements 
Backfill materials consisting of the on-site soils should be moisture-conditioned to 
at least 2 percent above the optimum moisture, placed in level lifts not exceeding 8 inches 
in loose thickness, and compacted to a minimum of 90 percent relative compaction. The 
SECTION 3. DISCUSSION AND RECOMMENDATIONS  
W.O. 8155-00 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 13 
Hawaii • California 
upper 3 feet below the pavement grade should be compacted to a minimum of 95 percent 
relative compaction. 
Imported fill materials should be moisture-conditioned to above the optimum 
moisture, placed in level lifts of about 8 inches in loose thickness, and compacted to a 
minimum of 95 percent relative compaction. Aggregate base course and subbase 
materials should be moisture-conditioned to above the optimum moisture content, placed 
in level lifts not exceeding 8 inches in loose thickness, and compacted to a minimum of 
95 percent relative compaction. Relative compaction refers to the in-place dry density of 
soil expressed as a percentage of the maximum dry density of the same soil established 
in accordance with ASTM D1557. Optimum moisture is the water content (percentage by 
dry weight) corresponding to the maximum dry density. 
Compaction should be accomplished by sheepsfoot rollers, vibratory rollers, or 
other types of acceptable compaction equipment. Water tamping, jetting, or ponding 
should not be allowed. 
Geolabs should perform observations and soil density tests during backfilling to 
assist the contractor in obtaining the required degree of compaction and the proper 
moisture content. Where compaction is less than required, additional compactive effort 
should be applied with adjustment of moisture content as necessary to obtain the 
specified compaction. 
3.6 
Pavement Restoration 
It is anticipated that asphaltic concrete pavement will be used to repave the top of 
the trenches along the water main alignments within the roadway. In general, we 
anticipate the vehicle loading for these pavements will primarily consist of passenger 
vehicles, light pick-up trucks, City and County of Honolulu buses, school buses, and 
refuse vehicles. 
We have assumed that the pavement subgrade will consist of the excavated 
on-site clayey soils encountered near the surface. In general, the new pavements along 
the waterline trenches should match the existing pavement sections or the following 
flexible sections may be used, whichever is greater. 
SECTION 3. DISCUSSION AND RECOMMENDATIONS

California Bearing Ratio (CBR) and field density tests should be performed on the actual 
subgrade soils encountered during construction to confirm the adequacy of the above 
section. 
Asphalt concrete base (ACB) material should consist of asphalt-treated basalt 
aggregate, placed in a layer not to exceed 6 inches in compacted thickness, and 
compacted to no less than 92 percent of the maximum theoretical specific gravity 
determined in accordance with AASHTO T-209 or ASTM D2041. Asphalt concrete (AC) 
should consist of Mix #4 in accordance with the Standard Specifications for Public Works 
Construction (September 1986) within City jurisdiction and in accordance with the Hawaii 
Standard Specifications for Road and Bridge Construction, 2005 (HSS) within State 
jurisdiction. 
Asphalt concrete base (ACB) and AC material within City jurisdiction should be 
constructed in accordance with Sections 32 and 34 of the Standard Specifications for 
Public Works Construction (September 1986), respectively. 
Aggregate base course as required within the City jurisdiction should meet the 
material requirements for Base Course as specified in Section 31 of the Standard

3.7 
Valve Vault Foundations 
We envision that concrete valve structures may be required for the proposed 
waterlines. Based on the subsurface conditions encountered at the site, we recommend 
an allowable bearing pressure of up to 2,500 pounds per square foot (psf) for foundations 
bearing on the underlying fills, residual and saprolitic soils, alluvium, cobbles, boulders, 
and basalt formation. This bearing value is for dead-plus-live loads and may be increased 
by one-third (⅓) for transient loads, such as those caused by seismic forces. 
If foundations are designed and constructed in accordance with our 
recommendations, we estimate the total settlements of the valve vault structure to be less 
than 1 inch. 
Lateral loads acting on the valve vault structure may be resisted by friction between 
the base of the foundation and on-site soils or select granular fill and by passive earth 
pressure developed against the near vertical faces of the embedded valve vault. 
A coefficient of friction of 0.35 may be used for foundations bearing on the compacted 
on-site soils. Resistance to lateral loads due to passive pressure may be estimated using 
an equivalent fluid pressure of 300 pounds per square foot per foot of depth (pcf). Unless 
covered by pavements or slabs, the passive resistance in the upper 12 inches below 
finished grade should be neglected. 
The valve vault structure should be designed to resist the lateral earth pressure 
due to the adjacent soils and surcharge effects. The following lateral earth pressure, 
expressed in equivalent fluid pressures of pounds per square foot per foot of depth (pcf), 
may be used in the design of the valve vault walls. 
SECTION 3. DISCUSSION AND RECOMMENDATIONS

The value provided above assumes that non-expansive, select granular fill 
materials will be used to backfill behind the valve vault structures. It is assumed that the 
backfill behind vault wall structures will be compacted to between 90 and 95 percent 
relative compaction. Over-compaction of the vault wall structure backfill should be 
avoided. 
Surcharge stresses due to areal surcharges, traffic loads, line loads, and point 
loads within a horizontal distance equal to the depth of the wall should be considered in 
the design. For uniform surcharge stresses imposed on the loaded side of the wall, a 
rectangular distribution with a uniform pressure equal to 53 percent of the vertical 
surcharge pressure acting on the entire height of the wall should be used. Additional 
analyses during design may be needed to evaluate the surcharge effects of point loads 
and line loads. 
3.8 
Thrust Blocks 
We envision that thrust blocks will be required at some locations to provide 
resistance to the thrust forces due to the pressurized pipes. The thrust force acting on the 
pipes may be resisted by passive earth pressure acting against the face of the thrust 
block. We anticipate that most of the planned inverts will be about 4 to 5 feet below the 
existing pavement surface. However, some of the thrust blocks may need to be located 
at deeper depths in areas where the new water main traverses existing utilities. 
Based on our field exploration, variable materials ranging from stiff to hard clayey 
silt and silty clay to dense to very dense silty sand, gravely sand, sandy gravel, and basalt 
rock formation were encountered within the depths explored. We recommend using a 
lateral bearing pressure of 1,000 pounds per square foot (psf) for the design of thrust 
blocks bearing against the in-situ materials underlying the site.  
SECTION 3. DISCUSSION AND RECOMMENDATIONS  
W.O. 8155-00 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 17 
Hawaii • California 
3.9 
Settlements 
Primary settlement of the water mains is normally caused by the difference in the 
unit weight of the lighter excavated original earth and the heavier compacted backfill 
material placed over the pipes. The net increase in loading will cause settlement of the 
underlying subsoils below the trench invert. Based on our engineering analyses, 
settlement on the order of less than ½ inch is anticipated for the project. 
The above estimate assumes that proper construction procedures and good 
workmanship will be engaged during construction. Additional settlement could occur if 
improper trench support is used. 
3.10 Corrosion Potential 
Design of metallic substructures, such as waterline pipes or other metallic piping, 
should consider the effects of the corrosive environment on the substructure. Forty-seven 
sets of laboratory corrosion tests, including pH, minimum resistivity, chloride content, and 
sulfate content, were performed on selected samples obtained during our field exploration 
to evaluate the corrosivity of the near-surface soils at the project site. The test results are 
summarized on Plates B-17 and B-18 in Appendix B. The soil resistivity was determined 
in general accordance with ASTM G57.  
Based on the updated version of the Board of Water Supply, City and County of 
Honolulu Water System External Corrosion Control Standards dated July 28, 2021, there 
are two soil corrosivity categories. Category A (Moderately to Severely Corrosive) applies 
to soil meeting any one of the following conditions. Corrosion Category B (Negligibly to 
Mildly Corrosive) is applicable when none of the following conditions are met. The 
updated version of the standards is effective as of the publishing date for Oahu only. 
• Resistivity ≤ 5,000 ohm-cm 
• pH ≤ 6.5 
• Water-soluble chloride concentration ≥ 500 ppm 
• Water soluble sulfate concentration ≥ 1,000 ppm 
• Within fluctuating saltwater table 
Resistivity is generally recognized as one of the most significant soil characteristics 
regarding the corrosivity of the soil to buried metallic objects. In general, the lower the 
resistivity, the greater the potential for corrosion of the buried metallic structure. 
SECTION 3. DISCUSSION AND RECOMMENDATIONS  
W.O. 8155-00 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 18 
Hawaii • California 
Conversely, the higher the resistivity, the less likely the soil will contribute to the corrosion 
of metallic objects. Results of the resistivity testing indicate that the on-site soils have 
resistivity values ranging from 550 to 4,800 ohm-cm with pH values varying from 5.7 to 
8.79. Therefore, the on-site near-surface soils may be considered as Category A 
(Moderately to Severely Corrosive) based on the updated Board of Water Supply External 
Corrosion Control Standards dated July 28, 2021. 
In addition, chloride content and sulfate content testing were performed by 
TestAmerica Laboratories, Inc. to evaluate the corrosivity of the on-site soils encountered. 
Based on the chloride and sulfate content tests performed on the on-site soils, the test 
values are in the range of not detected to 74 ppm for chloride content and not detected 
to 480 ppm for sulfate content. It may be appropriate to consult with a professional 
corrosion engineer to review the test results and provide detailed recommendations for 
corrosion protection. Detailed results of the Chloride Content (EPA 300.0) and Sulfate 
Content (EPA 300.0) testing, performed by TestAmerica Laboratories, Inc., are presented 
in Appendix C.  
3.11 Design Review 
Drawings and specifications for the proposed construction should be forwarded to 
Geolabs for review and written comments prior to bid advertisement. This review is 
necessary to evaluate the conformance of the plans and specifications with the intent of 
the recommendations provided herein. If this review is not made, Geolabs cannot assume 
responsibility for misinterpretation of the recommendations presented. 
3.12 Construction Observation 
We recommend retaining Geolabs for geotechnical engineering services during 
construction. The critical items of construction monitoring that require "Special Inspection" 
include periodic observation of the trench excavation and placement of bedding materials 
and trench backfill, and pavement restoration subgrade preparation. 
A Geolabs representative should also monitor other aspects of earthwork 
construction to observe compliance with the design concepts, specifications, or 
recommendations, and to expedite suggestions for design changes that may be required 
in the event that subsurface conditions differ from those anticipated at the time this report

was prepared. Geolabs should be accorded the opportunity to provide geotechnical

SECTION 4.  LIMITATIONS 
The analyses and recommendations submitted herein are based in part upon 
information obtained from the field borings. Variations of conditions beyond the field 
borings may occur, and the nature and extent of these variations may not become evident 
until construction is underway. If variations then appear evident, it will be necessary to 
re-evaluate the recommendations presented herein. 
The field boring locations are approximate, having been estimated by taping from 
reference points and visible features shown on the topographic survey maps transmitted 
by G70 on November 30, 2020. Elevations of the borings were interpolated based on the 
spot elevations shown on the same plans. The physical locations and elevations of the 
borings should be considered accurate only to the degree implied by the methods used. 
The stratification lines shown on the graphic representations of the borings depict 
the approximate boundaries between soil and/or rock types and, as such, may denote a 
gradual transition. Water level data from the borings were measured at the times shown 
on the graphic representation and/or presented in the text herein. These data have been 
reviewed and interpretations made in the formulation of this report. However, it must be 
noted that fluctuation may occur due to variation in rainfall, temperature, and other factors. 
This report has been prepared for the exclusive use of G70 for specific application 
to the proposed Leolua Street and Kipou Street Area Water System Improvements project 
as described herein in accordance with generally accepted geotechnical engineering 
principles and practices. No warranty is expressed or implied. 
The owner/client should be aware that unanticipated soil conditions are commonly 
encountered. Unforeseen subsurface conditions, such as perched groundwater, soft 
deposits, and hard layers or cavities, may occur in localized areas and may require 
additional probing or corrections in the field (which may result in construction delays) to 
attain a properly constructed project. Therefore, a sufficient contingency fund is 
recommended to accommodate these possible extra costs. 
SECTION 4. LIMITATIONS

GEOLABS, INC.

Hawaii • California

intended to investigate the potential presence of hazardous materials existing at the site. 
It should be noted that the equipment, techniques, and personnel used to conduct a 
geo-environmental exploration differ substantially from those applied in geotechnical 
engineering.

A P P E N D I X   A 
Field Exploration 
We explored the subsurface conditions by drilling and sampling 47 borings, 
designated as Boring Nos. 1 through 47, extending to depths of about 10 to 11.5 feet 
below the existing pavement surface. In addition, five bulk samples of the near-surface 
soils, designated as Bulk-1 through Bulk-5, were obtained to evaluate the pavement 
support characteristics of the near-surface soils. The approximate boring and bulk sample 
locations are shown on the Overall Site Plan, Plate 2, and Site Plans, Plates 3.1 through 
3.4. The borings were drilled using a truck-mounted drill rig equipped with continuous 
flight augers. 
Our geologist classified the materials encountered in the borings by visual and 
textural examination in the field in general accordance with ASTM D2488, Standard 
Practice for Description and Identification of Soils, and monitored the drilling operations 
on a near-continuous (full-time) basis. These classifications were further reviewed visually 
and by testing in the laboratory. Soils were classified in general accordance with 
ASTM D2487, Standard Practice for Classification of Soils for Engineering Purposes 
(Unified Soil Classification System), as shown on the Soil Log Legend, Plate A-0.1. 
Deviations made to the soil classification in accordance with ASTM D2487 are described 
in the Soil Classification Log Key, Plate A-0.2. Graphic representations of the materials 
encountered are presented on the Logs of Borings, Plates A-1 through A-47. 
Relatively “undisturbed” soil samples were obtained in general accordance with 
ASTM D3550, Ring-Lined Barrel Sampling of Soils, by driving a 3-inch OD Modified 
California sampler with a 140-pound hammer falling 30 inches. In addition, some samples 
were obtained from the borings in general accordance with ASTM D1586, Penetration 
Test and Split-Barrel Sampling of Soils, by driving a 2-inch OD standard penetration 
sampler using the same hammer and drop. The blow counts needed to drive the sampler 
the second and third 6 inches of an 18-inch drive are shown as the “Penetration 
Resistance” on the Logs of Borings at the appropriate sample depths. The penetration 
resistance shown on the Logs of Borings indicates the number of blows required for the 
specific sampler type used. The blow counts may need to be factored to obtain the 
Standard Penetration Test (SPT) blow counts. 
Pocket penetrometer tests were performed on selected cohesive soil samples 
retrieved in the field. The pocket penetrometer test provides an indication of the 
unconfined compressive strength of the sample. Pocket penetrometer test results are 
summarized on the Logs of Borings at the appropriate sample depths.

COHESIVE SOIL (- #200    50%)
PRIMARY constituents are composed of the largest
percent of the soil mass. Primary constituents are
capitalized and bold (i.e., GRAVEL, SAND)
PRIMARY constituents are based on plasticity. Primary
constituents are capitalized and bold (i.e., CLAY, SILT)
SECONDARY constituents are composed of a
percentage less than the primary constituent. If the soil
mass consists of 12 percent or more fines content, a
cohesive constituent is used (SILTY or CLAYEY);
otherwise, a granular constituent is used (GRAVELLY
or SANDY) provided that the secondary constituent
consists of 20 percent or more of the soil mass.
Secondary constituents are capitalized and bold (i.e.,
SANDY GRAVEL, CLAYEY SAND) and precede the
primary constituent.
SECONDARY constituents are composed of a
percentage less than the primary constituent, but more
than 20 percent of the soil mass. Secondary constituents
are capitalized and bold (i.e., SANDY CLAY, SILTY
CLAY, CLAYEY SILT) and precede the primary
constituent.

Rock Log Legend
The following terms describe the resistance of a rock to indentation or scratching:
Specimen breaks with difficulty after several "pinging" hammer blows.
Example: Dense, fine grain volcanic rock
Specimen breaks with some difficulty after several hammer blows.
Example: Vesicular, vugular, coarse-grained rock
Specimen can be broked by one hammer blow. Cannot be scraped by knife. SPT may penetrate by
~25 blows per inch with bounce.
Example: Porous rock such as clinker, cinder, and coral reef
Can be indented by one hammer blow. Can be scraped or peeled by knife. SPT can penetrate by
~100 blows per foot.
Example: Weathered rock, chalk-like coral reef
Crumbles under hammer blow. Can be peeled and carved by knife. Can be indented by finger
pressure.
Example: Saprolite
CONGLOMERATE

APPENDIX B
W.O. 8155-00 
GEOLABS, INC. 
SEPTEMBER 2021   Page B-1 
Hawaii • California 
A P P E N D I X   B 
Laboratory Tests 
Moisture Content (ASTM D2216) and Unit Weight determinations (ASTM D2937) 
were performed on selected soil samples as an aid in the classification and evaluation of 
soil properties. The test results are presented on the Logs of Borings at the appropriate 
sample depths. 
Nine Atterberg Limits tests (ASTM D4318) were performed on selected soil 
samples to evaluate the liquid and plastic limits. The test results are summarized on the 
Logs of Borings at the appropriate sample depths. Graphic presentations of the test 
results are provided on Plate B-1. 
Nine Sieve Analysis tests (ASTM C117 & C136) were performed on selected soil 
samples to evaluate the gradation characteristics of the soils and to aid in soil 
classification. Graphic presentations of the grain size distributions are provided on 
Plates B-2 and B-3. 
Four Direct Shear tests (ASTM D 3080) were performed on selected samples to 
evaluate the shear strength characteristics of the materials tested. The test results are 
presented on Plates B-4 through B-7. 
Six laboratory California Bearing Ratio tests (ASTM D1883) were performed on 
bulk samples of the near-surface soils to evaluate the pavement support characteristics 
of the soils. The test results are presented on Plates B-8 through B-13. 
Three Modified Proctor compaction tests (ASTM D 1557 A) were performed on 
bulk samples obtained to evaluate the relationships between the moisture content and 
dry density of the near-surface soils as fill materials. The test results are presented on 
Plates B-14 through B-16. 
Forty-seven pH (ASTM G51) and Minimum Resistivity (ASTM G57) tests were 
performed by our office, and Chloride (EPA 300) and Sulfate Content (EPA 300) tests 
were performed by TestAmerica Laboratories, Inc. on selected soil samples obtained 
from our field exploration. The test results are presented on Plates B-17 and B-18.

Definitions/Glossary
Job ID: 320-69710-1
Client: GeoLabs Inc
SDG: 8155-00
Project/Site: LEOLUA ST AND KIPOU ST AREA
Qualifiers
HPLC/IC
Qualifier Description
F1
MS and/or MSD recovery exceeds control limits.
Qualifier
H
Sample was prepped or analyzed beyond the specified holding time
H3
Sample was received and analyzed past holding time.
Glossary
These commonly used abbreviations may or may not be present in this report.
Listed under the "D" column to designate that the result is reported on a dry weight basis
Abbreviation
%R
Percent Recovery
CFL
Contains Free Liquid
CFU
Colony Forming Unit
CNF
Contains No Free Liquid
DER
Duplicate Error Ratio (normalized absolute difference)
Dil Fac
Dilution Factor
DL
Detection Limit (DoD/DOE)
DL, RA, RE, IN
Indicates a Dilution, Re-analysis, Re-extraction, or additional Initial metals/anion analysis of the sample
DLC
Decision Level Concentration (Radiochemistry)
EDL
Estimated Detection Limit (Dioxin)
LOD
Limit of Detection (DoD/DOE)
LOQ
Limit of Quantitation (DoD/DOE)
MCL
EPA recommended "Maximum Contaminant Level"
MDA
Minimum Detectable Activity (Radiochemistry)
MDC
Minimum Detectable Concentration (Radiochemistry)
MDL
Method Detection Limit
ML
Minimum Level (Dioxin)
MPN
Most Probable Number
MQL
Method Quantitation Limit
NC
Not Calculated
ND
Not Detected at the reporting limit (or MDL or EDL if shown)
NEG
Negative / Absent
POS
Positive / Present
PQL
Practical Quantitation Limit
PRES
Presumptive
QC
Quality Control
RER
Relative Error Ratio (Radiochemistry)
RL
Reporting Limit or Requested Limit (Radiochemistry)
RPD
Relative Percent Difference, a measure of the relative difference between two points
TEF
Toxicity Equivalent Factor (Dioxin)
TEQ
Toxicity Equivalent Quotient (Dioxin)
TNTC
Too Numerous To Count

Case Narrative
Client: GeoLabs Inc
Job ID: 320-69710-1
Project/Site: LEOLUA ST AND KIPOU ST AREA
SDG: 8155-00
Job ID: 320-69710-1
Laboratory: Eurofins TestAmerica, Sacramento
Narrative
Receipt 
The samples were received on 2/3/2021 9:30 AM. The temperature of the cooler at receipt was 15.7º C.
Receipt Exceptions
The samples were received at the laboratory outside holding time and the required temperature criteria (0 to 6º C.). 
Method 300.0
The following were diluted due to the nature of the sample matrix: B2 SPT2/RS3 2.5-6.5ft (320-69710-2), B3 RS1/SPT2 1-4ft 
(320-69710-3), B9 RS3 5-6.5ft (320-69710-9), (320-69710-A-2-B MS) and (320-69710-A-2-C MSD), preparation batch 320-460528 and 
analytical batch 320-463293. The samples were difficult to filter and were orange/brown in color after centrifuging. Attempts to run the 
samples at lower dilutions rendered the instrumentation inoperable. The samples were ran at 20x dilutions. Elevated reporting limits 
(RLs) are provided.
The following were diluted due to the nature of the sample matrix:  B2 SPT2/RS3 2.5-6.5ft (320-69710-2), (320-69710-A-2-B MS) and 
(320-69710-A-2-C MSD), analytical batch 320-463293. Because of this dilution, the matrix spike concentration in the MS/MSD was reduced 
to a level where the recovery calculation does not provide useful information.
The following were diluted due to the nature of the sample matrix: B1 RS1/SPT2 1-4ft (320-69710-1), B4 RS3/SPT4 5-11.5ft (320-69710-4), 
B5 RS1 1-2.5ft (320-69710-5), B6 RS1 1-2.5ft (320-69710-6), B7 RS3 5-6.5ft (320-69710-7), B8 RS1 1-2.5ft (320-69710-8), B10 RS3 
5-6.5ft (320-69710-10), B14 RS1 1-2.5ft (320-69710-14), (320-69710-A-14-A MS) and (320-69710-A-14-A MSD), preparation batch 
320-460528 and analytical batch 320-463433. The samples were difficult to filter and were orange/brown in color after centrifuging. 
Attempts to run the samples at lower dilutions rendered the instrumentation inoperable. The samples were ran at 20x dilutions. Elevated 
reporting limits (RLs) are provided.
The following were diluted due to the nature of the sample matrix: B11 RS3 5-6.5ft (320-69710-11), B12 RS1 1-2.5ft (320-69710-12), B13 
RS3 5-6.5ft (320-69710-13), B15 RS3 5-6.5ft (320-69710-15), B16 RS1 1-2.5ft (320-69710-16), B17 RS3 5-6.5ft (320-69710-17) and B18 
SPT2/RS3/SPT4 2.5-10.9ft (320-69710-18), preparation batch 320-460528 and analytical batch 320-464001. The samples were difficult to 
filter and were orange/brown in color after centrifuging. Attempts to run the samples at lower dilutions rendered the instrumentation 
inoperable. The samples were ran at 10x dilutions. Elevated reporting limits (RLs) are provided.
No additional analytical or quality issues were noted, other than those described above or in the Definitions/Glossary page.

Glossary
These commonly used abbreviations may or may not be present in this report.
Listed under the "D" column to designate that the result is reported on a dry weight basis
Abbreviation
%R
Percent Recovery
CFL
Contains Free Liquid
CFU
Colony Forming Unit
CNF
Contains No Free Liquid
DER
Duplicate Error Ratio (normalized absolute difference)
Dil Fac
Dilution Factor
DL
Detection Limit (DoD/DOE)
DL, RA, RE, IN
Indicates a Dilution, Re-analysis, Re-extraction, or additional Initial metals/anion analysis of the sample
DLC
Decision Level Concentration (Radiochemistry)
EDL
Estimated Detection Limit (Dioxin)
LOD
Limit of Detection (DoD/DOE)
LOQ
Limit of Quantitation (DoD/DOE)

EPA recommended "Maximum Contaminant Level"
MDA
Minimum Detectable Activity (Radiochemistry)

Method Detection Limit
ML
Minimum Level (Dioxin)
MPN
Most Probable Number
MQL
Method Quantitation Limit
NC
Not Calculated

Negative / Absent
POS
Positive / Present

Practical Quantitation Limit
PRES
Presumptive
QC
Quality Control

RL
Reporting Limit or Requested Limit (Radiochemistry)
RPD
Relative Percent Difference, a measure of the relative difference between two points

TEQ

Case Narrative
Client: GeoLabs Inc
Job ID: 320-69883-1
Project/Site: Leolua St and Kipou St
Job ID: 320-69883-1
Laboratory: Eurofins TestAmerica, Sacramento
Narrative
Receipt 
The samples were received on 2/8/2021 9:15 AM. The temperature of the cooler at receipt was 16.1º C.
Receipt Exceptions
The samples were received outside hold time and required temperature criteria (0 to 6º C.).
Sample collection times did not match (container versus Chain of Custody [COC]), respectively:
B-30 RS1 1-2.5FT (320-69883-1) - 10 AM versus 9 AM
B-31 RS1 1-2.5FT (320-69883-2) - 11 AM versus 10 AM
B-32 RS1 1-2.5FT (320-69883-3) - 11 AM versus 10 AM
B-33 RS3 5-6.5FT (320-69883-4) - 8 AM versus 10 AM
B-34 RS1/SPT2 1-4.5 (320-69883-5) - 9 AM versus 10 AM
B-35 RS1 1-.25FT (320-69883-6) - 11 AM versus 10 AM
B-36 RS1 1-2.5FT (320-69883-7) - 1 PM versus 9 AM
B-37 RS3 5-6.5FT (320-69883-8) - 8 AM versus 9 AM
B-38 SPT2/RS3 2.5-6.5FT (320-69883-9) - 10 AM versus 9 AM
B-39 RS1 1.5-3FT (320-69883-10) - 1 PM versus 9 AM
Collection dates (COC) read 12/21 (updated to 12/20). 
Sample ID did not match (container versus COC), respectively:
B-35 RS1 1-.25FT (320-69883-6) - B35/RS-1 1'-2.5' versus B-35 RS1 1-.25FT
B-38 SPT2/RS3 2.5-6.5FT (320-69883-9) - B38.SPT-2 RS-3 2.5'-6' versus B-38 SPT2/RS3 2.5-6.5FT
The samples were logged in per COC.
Method 300.0
The following were diluted due to the nature of the sample matrix: B-37 RS3 5-6.5FT (320-69883-8), (320-69883-A-8-B MS) and 
(320-69883-A-8-C MSD), analytical batch 320-463575. Elevated reporting limits (RLs) are provided. The samples were difficult to filter and 
orange/brown in color after centrifuging. Because of this dilution, the matrix spike concentration in the sample was reduced to a level 
where the recovery calculation does not provide useful information.
The following were diluted due to the nature of the sample matrix: B-34 RS1/SPT2 1-4.5 (320-69883-5), B-35 RS1 1-.25FT (320-69883-6), 
B-38 SPT2/RS3 2.5-6.5FT (320-69883-9) and B-39 RS1 1.5-3FT (320-69883-10), analytical batch 320-464001. Elevated reporting limits 
(RLs) are provided. The samples were extremely difficult to filter and were orange/brown in color after centrifuging (twice for twenty 
minutes at 3,800 RPM). The samples were orange/brown in color after filtration.
The following were diluted due to the nature of the sample matrix: B-32 RS1 1-2.5FT (320-69883-3) and B-33 RS3 5-6.5FT (320-69883-4), 
analytical batch 320-464256. Elevated reporting limits (RLs) are provided. The samples were difficult to filter/orange brown in color after 
centrifuging.
No additional analytical or quality issues were noted, other than those described above or in the Definitions/Glossary page.

Definitions/Glossary
Job ID: 320-70249-1
Client: GeoLabs Inc
Project/Site: Leolua St and Kipoi St
Qualifiers
HPLC/IC
Qualifier Description
4
MS, MSD: The analyte present in the original sample is greater than 4 times the matrix spike concentration; therefore, control limits are not 
applicable.
Qualifier
H
Sample was prepped or analyzed beyond the specified holding time
H3
Sample was received and analyzed past holding time.
Glossary
These commonly used abbreviations may or may not be present in this report.
Listed under the "D" column to designate that the result is reported on a dry weight basis
Abbreviation
%R
Percent Recovery
CFL
Contains Free Liquid
CFU
Colony Forming Unit
CNF
Contains No Free Liquid
DER
Duplicate Error Ratio (normalized absolute difference)
Dil Fac
Dilution Factor
DL
Detection Limit (DoD/DOE)
DL, RA, RE, IN
Indicates a Dilution, Re-analysis, Re-extraction, or additional Initial metals/anion analysis of the sample
DLC
Decision Level Concentration (Radiochemistry)
EDL
Estimated Detection Limit (Dioxin)
LOD
Limit of Detection (DoD/DOE)
LOQ
Limit of Quantitation (DoD/DOE)
MCL
EPA recommended "Maximum Contaminant Level"
MDA
Minimum Detectable Activity (Radiochemistry)
MDC
Minimum Detectable Concentration (Radiochemistry)
MDL
Method Detection Limit
ML
Minimum Level (Dioxin)
MPN
Most Probable Number
MQL
Method Quantitation Limit
NC
Not Calculated
ND
Not Detected at the reporting limit (or MDL or EDL if shown)
NEG
Negative / Absent
POS
Positive / Present
PQL
Practical Quantitation Limit
PRES
Presumptive
QC
Quality Control
RER
Relative Error Ratio (Radiochemistry)
RL
Reporting Limit or Requested Limit (Radiochemistry)
RPD
Relative Percent Difference, a measure of the relative difference between two points
TEF
Toxicity Equivalent Factor (Dioxin)
TEQ
Toxicity Equivalent Quotient (Dioxin)
TNTC
Too Numerous To Count

Case Narrative
Client: GeoLabs Inc
Job ID: 320-70249-1
Project/Site: Leolua St and Kipoi St
Job ID: 320-70249-1
Laboratory: Eurofins TestAmerica, Sacramento
Narrative
Receipt 
The samples were received on 2/18/2021 9:20 AM. The temperature of the cooler at receipt was 17.4º C.
Receipt Exceptions
The samples were received outside hold time and required temperature criteria (0 to 6º C.). Analyzing laboratory checklist incorrectly notes 
received with hold.
Sample collection times did not match (containers versus chain of custody): B-40 RS1 1-2.5FT (320-70249-1), B-41 RS1/SPT2 1-4FT 
(320-70249-2), B-42 RS1 1.25-2.75FT (320-70249-3), B-43 RS1 1.5-3FT (320-70249-4), B-44 RS1 1-2.5FT (320-70249-5), B-45 RS1 1-.25FT (320-70249-6), 
B-46 RS1 1-2.5FT (320-70249-7) and B-47 RS1/SPT2 1-3.5FT (320-70249-8). Container IDs missing "ft." 
The samples were logged in per chain of custody. 
Method 300.0
No analytical or quality issues were noted, other than those described in the Definitions/Glossary page.

Definitions/Glossary
Job ID: 320-69884-1
Client: GeoLabs Inc
Project/Site: Leolua St and Kipou St
Qualifiers
HPLC/IC
Qualifier Description
4
MS, MSD: The analyte present in the original sample is greater than 4 times the matrix spike concentration; therefore, control limits are not 
applicable.
Qualifier
H
Sample was prepped or analyzed beyond the specified holding time
H3
Sample was received and analyzed past holding time.
Glossary
These commonly used abbreviations may or may not be present in this report.
Listed under the "D" column to designate that the result is reported on a dry weight basis
Abbreviation
%R
Percent Recovery
CFL
Contains Free Liquid
CFU
Colony Forming Unit
CNF
Contains No Free Liquid
DER
Duplicate Error Ratio (normalized absolute difference)
Dil Fac
Dilution Factor
DL
Detection Limit (DoD/DOE)
DL, RA, RE, IN
Indicates a Dilution, Re-analysis, Re-extraction, or additional Initial metals/anion analysis of the sample
DLC
Decision Level Concentration (Radiochemistry)
EDL
Estimated Detection Limit (Dioxin)
LOD
Limit of Detection (DoD/DOE)
LOQ
Limit of Quantitation (DoD/DOE)
MCL
EPA recommended "Maximum Contaminant Level"
MDA
Minimum Detectable Activity (Radiochemistry)
MDC
Minimum Detectable Concentration (Radiochemistry)
MDL
Method Detection Limit
ML
Minimum Level (Dioxin)
MPN
Most Probable Number
MQL
Method Quantitation Limit
NC
Not Calculated
ND
Not Detected at the reporting limit (or MDL or EDL if shown)
NEG
Negative / Absent
POS
Positive / Present
PQL
Practical Quantitation Limit
PRES
Presumptive
QC
Quality Control
RER
Relative Error Ratio (Radiochemistry)
RL
Reporting Limit or Requested Limit (Radiochemistry)
RPD
Relative Percent Difference, a measure of the relative difference between two points
TEF
Toxicity Equivalent Factor (Dioxin)
TEQ
Toxicity Equivalent Quotient (Dioxin)
TNTC
Too Numerous To Count

Case Narrative
Client: GeoLabs Inc
Job ID: 320-69884-1
Project/Site: Leolua St and Kipou St
Job ID: 320-69884-1
Laboratory: Eurofins TestAmerica, Sacramento
Narrative
Receipt 
The samples were received on 2/8/2021 9:15 AM. The temperature of the cooler at receipt was 16.1º C.
Receipt Exceptions
The samples were received outside hold time and required temperature criteria (0 to 6º C.).
Sample collection times did not match (container versus Chain of Custody [COC]), respectively:
B-20 SPT2/RS3 2.5-6.5FT (320-69884-2) - 9 AM versus 10 AM
B-22 SPT2 2.5-4FT (320-69884-4) - 11 AM versus 10 AM
B-23 RS1/SPT2 1-4FT (320-69884-5) - 1 PM versus 10 AM
B-24 RS1 1-.25FT (320-69884-6) - 2 PM versus 10 AM
B-26 RS1-1-2.5FT (320-69884-8) - 10 AM versus 9 AM
B-27 SPT2/RS3 2.5-6.5FT (320-69884-9) - 11 AM versus 9 AM
B-29 RS1 1-2.5FT (320-69884-11) - 12 PM versus 10 AM
Sample ID did not match (container versus COC), respectively:
B-19 RS1 1-2.5FT (320-69884-1) - B-19/RS-1 1'-2' versus B-19 RS1 1-2.5FT
B-24 RS1 1-.25FT (320-69884-6) - B24/RS-1 1'-2.5' versus B-24 RS1 1-.25FT
The samples were logged in per COC.
Collection dates (COC) read 12/21 (updated to 12/20). 
Method 300.0
The following was diluted due to the nature of the sample matrix: B-28 RS3 5-6.5FT (320-69884-10), preparation batch 320-465506 and 
analytical batch 320-465736. Elevated reporting limits (RLs) are provided.
Due to the over-range concentration of sulfate in the parent sample, the matrix spike / matrix spike duplicate (MS/MSD) for preparation 
batch 320-465506 and analytical batch 320-465736 were also over the calibration range for sulfate and could not be evaluated for 
accuracy and precision. The associated laboratory control sample (LCS) met acceptance criteria.
The following samples were diluted to bring the concentration of target analytes within the calibration range: B-20 SPT2/RS3 2.5-6.5FT 
(320-69884-2), B-23 RS1/SPT2 1-4FT (320-69884-5), B-25 SPT2 2.5-4FT (320-69884-7), B-27 SPT2/RS3 2.5-6.5FT (320-69884-9), B-29 
RS1 1-2.5FT (320-69884-11), (320-69884-A-2-B MS), (320-69884-A-2-C MSD), (320-69884-A-11-B MS) and (320-69884-A-11-C MSD). 
Elevated reporting limits (RLs) are provided.
The matrix spike / matrix spike duplicate (MS/MSD) recoveries for sulfate were outside control limits: B-20 SPT2/RS3 2.5-6.5FT 
(320-69884-2), B-29 RS1 1-2.5FT (320-69884-11), (320-69884-A-2-B MS), (320-69884-A-2-C MSD), (320-69884-A-11-B MS) and 
(320-69884-A-11-C  MSD), preparation batch 320-465506 and analytical batch 320-466467. Sample matrix interference and/or 
non-homogeneity are suspected because the associated laboratory control sample (LCS) recovery was within acceptance limits.  
No additional analytical or quality issues were noted, other than those described above or in the Definitions/Glossary page.
Eurofins TestAmerica, Sacramento
